Title: John von Neumann

**John von Neumann** ( [_von NOY-mən_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key"); [Hungarian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hungarian_language "Hungarian language"): _Neumann János Lajos_[[ˈnɒjmɒn ˈjaːnoʃ ˈlɒjoʃ]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/Hungarian "Help:IPA/Hungarian"); December 28, 1903– February 8, 1957) was a Hungarian and American [mathematician](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematician "Mathematician"), [physicist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physicist "Physicist"), [computer scientist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_scientist "Computer scientist") and [engineer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Engineer "Engineer"). Von Neumann had perhaps the widest coverage of any mathematician of his time,[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTER%C3%A9dei19997-9) integrating [pure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basic_research "Basic research") and [applied sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Applied_science#Applied_research "Applied science") and making major contributions to many fields, including [mathematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics "Mathematics"), [physics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physics "Physics"), [economics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economics "Economics"), [computing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computing "Computing"), and [statistics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statistics "Statistics"). He was a pioneer in building the mathematical framework of [quantum physics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_physics "Quantum physics"), in the development of [functional analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Functional_analysis "Functional analysis"), and in [game theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Game_theory "Game theory"), introducing or codifying concepts including [cellular automata](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cellular_automaton "Cellular automaton"), the [universal constructor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von_Neumann_universal_constructor "Von Neumann universal constructor") and the [digital computer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer "Computer"). His analysis of the structure of [self-replication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-replication "Self-replication") preceded the discovery of the structure of [DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA "DNA").

During [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II "World War II"), von Neumann worked on the [Manhattan Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manhattan_Project "Manhattan Project"). He developed the mathematical models behind the [explosive lenses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Explosive_lens "Explosive lens") used in the [implosion-type nuclear weapon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Implosion-type_nuclear_weapon "Implosion-type nuclear weapon").[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992-10) Before and after the war, he consulted for many organizations including the [Office of Scientific Research and Development](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Office_of_Scientific_Research_and_Development "Office of Scientific Research and Development"), the [Army's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Army "United States Army")[Ballistic Research Laboratory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ballistic_Research_Laboratory "Ballistic Research Laboratory"), the [Armed Forces Special Weapons Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armed_Forces_Special_Weapons_Project "Armed Forces Special Weapons Project") and the [Oak Ridge National Laboratory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory "Oak Ridge National Laboratory").[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAspray1990246-11) At the peak of his influence in the 1950s, he chaired a number of [Defense Department](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense "United States Department of Defense") committees including the [Strategic Missile Evaluation Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Teapot_Committee "Teapot Committee") and the [ICBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ICBM "ICBM") Scientific Advisory Committee. He was also a member of the influential [Atomic Energy Commission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission "United States Atomic Energy Commission") in charge of all atomic energy development in the country. He played a key role alongside [Bernard Schriever](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernard_Schriever "Bernard Schriever") and [Trevor Gardner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trevor_Gardner "Trevor Gardner") in the design and development of the United States' first [ICBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile "Intercontinental ballistic missile") programs.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTESheehan2010-12) At that time he was considered the nation's foremost expert on [nuclear weaponry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_weapon "Nuclear weapon") and the leading defense scientist at the [U.S. Department of Defense](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense "United States Department of Defense").

Von Neumann's contributions and intellectual ability drew praise from colleagues in physics, mathematics, and beyond. Accolades he received range from the [Medal of Freedom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medal_of_Freedom_(1945) "Medal of Freedom (1945)") to a [crater on the Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von_Neumann_(crater) "Von Neumann (crater)") named in his honor.

Von Neumann was born in [Budapest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Budapest "Budapest"), Kingdom of Hungary (then part of Austria-Hungary),[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-13)[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-Time-14)[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlair1957104-15) on December 28, 1903, to a wealthy, non-observant [Jewish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish "Jewish") family. His birth name was **Neumann János Lajos**. In Hungarian, the family name comes first, and his given names are equivalent to John Louis in English.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhattacharya20224-16)

He was the eldest of three brothers; his two younger siblings were Mihály (Michael) and Miklós (Nicholas).[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDyson1998xxi-17) His father Neumann Miksa (Max von Neumann) was a banker and held a [doctorate in law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doctor_of_law "Doctor of law"). He had moved to Budapest from [Pécs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/P%C3%A9cs "Pécs") at the end of the 1880s.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199238%E2%80%9342-18) Miksa's father and grandfather were born in Ond (now part of [Szerencs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Szerencs "Szerencs")), [Zemplén County](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zempl%C3%A9n_County "Zemplén County"), northern Hungary. John's mother was Kann Margit (Margaret Kann);[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199237%E2%80%9338-19) her parents were Kann Jákab and Meisels Katalin of the [Meisels family](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meisel_family "Meisel family").[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199239-20) Three generations of the Kann family lived in spacious apartments above the Kann-Heller offices in Budapest; von Neumann's family occupied an 18-room apartment on the top floor.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199244%E2%80%9345-21)

On February 20, 1913, [Emperor Franz Joseph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emperor_Franz_Joseph "Emperor Franz Joseph") elevated John's father to the Hungarian nobility for his service to the Austro-Hungarian Empire.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-archives.hungaricana.hu-22) The Neumann family thus acquired the hereditary appellation _Margittai_, meaning "of Margitta" (today [Marghita](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marghita "Marghita"), Romania). The family had no connection with the town; the appellation was chosen in reference to Margaret, as was their chosen [coat of arms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coat_of_arms "Coat of arms") depicting three [marguerites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argyranthemum "Argyranthemum"). Neumann János became margittai Neumann János (John Neumann de Margitta), which he later changed to the German Johann von Neumann.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199257%E2%80%9358-23)

Von Neumann was a [child prodigy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Child_prodigy "Child prodigy") who at six years old could divide two eight-digit numbers in his head[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-24)[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchneiderGerstingBrinkman201528-25) and converse in [Ancient Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek "Ancient Greek").[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-26) He, his brothers and his cousins were instructed by governesses. Von Neumann's father believed that knowledge of languages other than their native [Hungarian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hungarian_language "Hungarian language") was essential, so the children were tutored in [English](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_(language) "English (language)"), [French](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_(language) "French (language)"), [German](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_(language) "German (language)") and [Italian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_(language) "Italian (language)").[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199246%E2%80%9347-27) By age eight, von Neumann was familiar with [differential](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_calculus "Differential calculus") and [integral calculus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral_calculus "Integral calculus"), and by twelve he had read [Borel's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%89mile_Borel "Émile Borel")_La Théorie des Fonctions_.[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalmos1973383-28) He was also interested in history, reading [Wilhelm Oncken](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Oncken "Wilhelm Oncken")'s 46-volume world history series _Allgemeine Geschichte in Einzeldarstellungen_ (_General History in Monographs_).[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlair195790-29) One of the rooms in the apartment was converted into a library and reading room.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199252-30)

Von Neumann entered the Lutheran [Fasori Evangélikus Gimnázium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fasori_Gimn%C3%A1zium "Fasori Gimnázium") in 1914.[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAspray1990-31)[Eugene Wigner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eugene_Wigner "Eugene Wigner") was a year ahead of von Neumann at the school and soon became his friend.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199270%E2%80%9371-32)

Although von Neumann's father insisted that he attend school at the grade level appropriate to his age, he agreed to hire private tutors to give von Neumann advanced instruction. At 15, he began to study advanced calculus under the analyst [Gábor Szegő](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%A1bor_Szeg%C5%91 "Gábor Szegő").[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199270%E2%80%9371-32) On their first meeting, Szegő was so astounded by von Neumann's mathematical talent and speed that, as recalled by his wife, he came back home with tears in his eyes.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-33) By 19, von Neumann had published two major mathematical papers, the second of which gave the modern definition of [ordinal numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ordinal_number#Von_Neumann_definition_of_ordinals "Ordinal number"), which superseded [Georg Cantor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Cantor "Georg Cantor")'s definition.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-Nasar-p81-34) At the conclusion of his education at the gymnasium, he applied for and won the Eötvös Prize, a national award for mathematics.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199284-35)

According to his friend [Theodore von Kármán](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theodore_von_K%C3%A1rm%C3%A1n "Theodore von Kármán"), von Neumann's father wanted John to follow him into industry, and asked von Kármán to persuade his son not to take mathematics.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-36) Von Neumann and his father decided that the best career path was [chemical engineering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_engineer "Chemical engineer"). This was not something that von Neumann had much knowledge of, so it was arranged for him to take a two-year, non-degree course in chemistry at the [University of Berlin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Berlin "University of Berlin"), after which he sat for the entrance exam to [ETH Zurich](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ETH_Zurich "ETH Zurich"),[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199285%E2%80%9387-37) which he passed in September 1923.[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199297-38) Simultaneously von Neumann entered [Pázmány Péter University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E%C3%B6tv%C3%B6s_Lor%C3%A1nd_University "Eötvös Loránd University"), then known as the University of Budapest, as a [Ph.D.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy "Doctor of Philosophy") candidate in [mathematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics "Mathematics").[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-NYT-39) For his thesis, he produced an [axiomatization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiomatization "Axiomatization") of [Cantor's set theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Cantor#Set_theory "Georg Cantor").[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-Neumann1928-40)[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199286%E2%80%9387-41) In 1926, he graduated as a [chemical engineer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_engineer "Chemical engineer") from ETH Zurich and simultaneously passed his final examinations _[summa cum laude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Summa\_cum\_laude "Summa cum laude")_ for his Ph.D. in mathematics (with minors in [experimental physics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Experimental_physics "Experimental physics") and chemistry) at the University of Budapest.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-42)[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPais2000187-43)

He then went to the [University of Göttingen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_G%C3%B6ttingen "University of Göttingen") on a grant from the [Rockefeller Foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rockefeller_Foundation "Rockefeller Foundation") to study mathematics under [David Hilbert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Hilbert "David Hilbert").[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199298%E2%80%9399-44)[Hermann Weyl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermann_Weyl "Hermann Weyl") remembers how in the winter of 1926–1927 von Neumann, [Emmy Noether](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emmy_Noether "Emmy Noether"), and he would walk through "the cold, wet, rain-wet streets of [Göttingen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen "Göttingen")" after class discussing [hypercomplex number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypercomplex_number "Hypercomplex number") systems and their [representations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Representation_theory "Representation theory").[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-45)

Career and private life
-----------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=John_von_Neumann&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: Career and private life")]

[![Image 3](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/NeumannVonMargitta.jpg/500px-NeumannVonMargitta.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NeumannVonMargitta.jpg)

Excerpt from the university calendars for 1928 and 1928/29 of the [Friedrich-Wilhelms-Universität Berlin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humboldt_University_of_Berlin "Humboldt University of Berlin") announcing Neumann's lectures on the theory of functions II, axiomatic set theory and mathematical logic, the mathematical colloquium, review of recent work in quantum mechanics, special functions of mathematical physics and Hilbert's proof theory. He also lectured on the theory of relativity, set theory, integral equations and analysis of infinitely many variables.

Von Neumann's [habilitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habilitation "Habilitation") was completed on December 13, 1927, and he began to give lectures as a _[Privatdozent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Privatdozent "Privatdozent")_ at the University of Berlin in 1928.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-46) He was the youngest person elected _Privatdozent_ in the university's history.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-47) He began writing nearly one major mathematics paper per month.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992145-48) In 1929, he briefly became a _Privatdozent_ at the [University of Hamburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Hamburg "University of Hamburg"), where the prospects of becoming a tenured professor were better,[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992143%E2%80%93144-49) then in October of that year moved to [Princeton University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Princeton_University "Princeton University") as a visiting lecturer in [mathematical physics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_physics "Mathematical physics").[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992155%E2%80%93157-50)

Von Neumann was baptized a Catholic in 1930.[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBochner1958446-51) Shortly afterward, he married Marietta Kövesi, who had studied economics at Budapest University.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992155%E2%80%93157-50) Von Neumann and Marietta had a daughter, [Marina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marina_von_Neumann_Whitman "Marina von Neumann Whitman"), born in 1935; she would become a professor.[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-52) The couple divorced on November 2, 1937.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-53) On November 17, 1938, von Neumann married [Klára Dán](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Klara_Dan_von_Neumann "Klara Dan von Neumann").[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeims1980178-54)[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992170%E2%80%93174-55)

In 1933 Von Neumann accepted a tenured professorship at the [Institute for Advanced Study](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Institute_for_Advanced_Study "Institute for Advanced Study") in New Jersey, when that institution's plan to appoint [Hermann Weyl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermann_Weyl "Hermann Weyl") appeared to have failed.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992167%E2%80%93168-56) His mother, brothers and in-laws followed von Neumann to the United States in 1939.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992195%E2%80%93196-57) Von Neumann [anglicized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglicisation "Anglicisation") his name to John, keeping the German-aristocratic surname [von](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von "Von") Neumann.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199257%E2%80%9358-23) Von Neumann became a [naturalized U.S. citizen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naturalization#United_States "Naturalization") in 1937, and immediately tried to become a [lieutenant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lieutenant "Lieutenant") in the U.S. Army's [Officers Reserve Corps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Officers_Reserve_Corps "Officers Reserve Corps"). He passed the exams but was rejected because of his age.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992190%E2%80%93195-58)

Klára and John von Neumann were socially active within the local academic community.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992170%E2%80%93171-59) His white [clapboard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clapboard "Clapboard") house on Westcott Road was one of Princeton's largest private residences.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-60) He always wore formal suits.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-whitman-61) He enjoyed [Yiddish](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_humour "Jewish humour") and ["off-color"](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Off-color_humor "Off-color humor") humor.[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalmos1973383-28) In Princeton, he received complaints for playing extremely loud German [march music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/March_(music) "March (music)");[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199248-62) Von Neumann did some of his best work in noisy, chaotic environments.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlair195794-63) According to [Churchill Eisenhart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Churchill_Eisenhart "Churchill Eisenhart"), von Neumann could attend parties until the early hours of the morning and then deliver a lecture at 8:30.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-64)

He was known for always being happy to provide others of all ability levels with scientific and mathematical advice.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-halperin1990-4)[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldstine19857-65)[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-66) Wigner wrote that he perhaps supervised more work (in a casual sense) than any other modern mathematician.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTESzanton1992227-67) His daughter wrote that he was very concerned with his legacy in two aspects: his life and the durability of his intellectual contributions to the world.[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-68)

Many considered him an excellent chairman of committees, deferring rather easily on personal or organizational matters but pressing on technical ones. [Herbert York](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbert_York "Herbert York") described the many "Von Neumann Committees" that he participated in as "remarkable in style as well as output". The way the committees von Neumann chaired worked directly and intimately with the necessary military or corporate entities became a blueprint for all [Air Force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Air_Force "United States Air Force") long-range missile programs.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYork197118-69) Many people who had known von Neumann were puzzled by his relationship to the military and to power structures in general.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPais2006108-70)[Stanisław Ulam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_Ulam "Stanisław Ulam") suspected that he had a hidden admiration for people or organizations that could influence the thoughts and decision making of others.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUlam1976231%E2%80%93232-71)

He also maintained his knowledge of languages learnt in his youth. He knew Hungarian, French, German and English fluently, and maintained a conversational level of Italian, Yiddish, Latin and Ancient Greek. His Spanish was less perfect.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUlam19585%E2%80%936-72) He had a passion for and encyclopedic knowledge of ancient history,[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTESzanton1992277-73)[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlair195793-74) and he enjoyed reading [Ancient Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greece "Ancient Greece") historians in the original Greek. Ulam suspected they may have shaped his views on how future events could play out and how human nature and society worked in general.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUlam197697,_102,_244%E2%80%93245-75)

Von Neumann's closest friend in the United States was the mathematician [Stanisław Ulam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_Ulam "Stanisław Ulam").[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-76) Von Neumann believed that much of his mathematical thought occurred intuitively; he would often go to sleep with a problem unsolved and know the answer upon waking up.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlair195794-63) Ulam noted that von Neumann's way of thinking might not be visual, but more aural.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae199275-77) Ulam recalled, "Quite independently of his liking for abstract wit, he had a strong appreciation (one might say almost a hunger) for the more earthy type of comedy and humor".[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUlam19584%E2%80%936-78)

[![Image 4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/John_von_neumann_tomb_2004.jpg/250px-John_von_neumann_tomb_2004.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:John_von_neumann_tomb_2004.jpg)

Von Neumann's gravestone

In 1955, a mass was found near von Neumann's collarbone, which turned out to be cancer originating in the [skeleton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bone_tumor "Bone tumor"), [pancreas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pancreatic_cancer "Pancreatic cancer") or [prostate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prostate_cancer "Prostate cancer"). (While there is general agreement that the tumor had [metastasised](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metastasis "Metastasis"), sources differ on the location of the primary cancer.)[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-79)[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-80) The malignancy may have been caused by [exposure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absorbed_dose "Absorbed dose") to [radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ionizing_radiation "Ionizing radiation") at [Los Alamos National Laboratory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory "Los Alamos National Laboratory").[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsen201562-81) As death neared he asked for a priest, though the priest later recalled that von Neumann found little comfort in receiving the [last rites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_rites "Last rites")– he remained terrified of death and unable to accept it.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-82)[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalmos1973383,_394-83)[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobsen201563-84)[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-85) Of his religious views, Von Neumann reportedly said, "So long as there is the possibility of eternal damnation for nonbelievers it is more logical to be a believer at the end," referring to [Pascal's wager](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascal%27s_wager "Pascal's wager"). He confided to his mother, "There probably has to be a God. Many things are easier to explain if there is than if there isn't."[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-86)[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-87)

He died Roman Catholic[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBochner1958446-51) on February 8, 1957, aged 53, at [Walter Reed Army Medical Hospital](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Reed_Army_Medical_Hospital "Walter Reed Army Medical Hospital") and was buried at [Princeton Cemetery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Princeton_Cemetery "Princeton Cemetery").[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992380-88)[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-89)

[![Image 5](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/NBG_Evolution_svg.svg/500px-NBG_Evolution_svg.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NBG_Evolution_svg.svg)

History of approaches that led to NBG set theory

At the beginning of the 20th century, efforts to base mathematics on [naive set theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naive_set_theory "Naive set theory") suffered a setback due to [Russell's paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russell%27s_paradox "Russell's paradox") (on the set of all sets that do not belong to themselves).[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992104%E2%80%93105-90) The problem of an adequate axiomatization of [set theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Set_theory "Set theory") was resolved implicitly about twenty years later by [Ernst Zermelo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernst_Zermelo "Ernst Zermelo") and [Abraham Fraenkel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abraham_Fraenkel "Abraham Fraenkel"). [Zermelo–Fraenkel set theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zermelo%E2%80%93Fraenkel_set_theory "Zermelo–Fraenkel set theory") provided a series of principles that allowed for the construction of the sets used in the everyday practice of mathematics, but did not explicitly exclude the possibility of the existence of a set that belongs to itself. In his 1925 doctoral thesis, von Neumann demonstrated two techniques to exclude such sets—the _[axiom of foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom\_of\_regularity "Axiom of regularity")_ and the notion of _[class](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Class\_(set\_theory) "Class (set theory)")._[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-vanheijenoort-91)

The axiom of foundation proposed that every set can be constructed from the bottom up in an ordered succession of steps by way of the Zermelo–Fraenkel principles. If one set belongs to another, then the first must necessarily come before the second in the succession. This excludes the possibility of a set belonging to itself. To demonstrate that the addition of this new axiom to the others did not produce contradictions, von Neumann introduced the _method of [inner models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inner\_model "Inner model")_, which became an essential demonstration instrument in set theory.[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-vanheijenoort-91)

The second approach to the problem of sets belonging to themselves took as its base the notion of [class](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Class_(set_theory) "Class (set theory)"), and defines a set as a class that belongs to other classes, while a _proper class_ is defined as a class that does not belong to other classes. On the Zermelo–Fraenkel approach, the axioms impede the construction of a set of all sets that do not belong to themselves. In contrast, on von Neumann's approach, the class of all sets that do not belong to themselves can be constructed, but it is a _proper class_, not a set.[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-vanheijenoort-91)

Overall, von Neumann's major achievement in set theory was an "axiomatization of set theory and (connected with that) elegant theory of the [ordinal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ordinal_number "Ordinal number") and [cardinal numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardinal_number "Cardinal number") as well as the first strict formulation of principles of definitions by the [transfinite induction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transfinite_induction "Transfinite induction")".[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurawski2010196-92)

#### Von Neumann paradox

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=John_von_Neumann&action=edit&section=9 "Edit section: Von Neumann paradox")]

Building on the [Hausdorff paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hausdorff_paradox "Hausdorff paradox") of [Felix Hausdorff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Felix_Hausdorff "Felix Hausdorff") (1914), [Stefan Banach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stefan_Banach "Stefan Banach") and [Alfred Tarski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_Tarski "Alfred Tarski") in 1924 showed how to subdivide a three-dimensional [ball](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ball_(mathematics) "Ball (mathematics)") into [disjoint sets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disjoint_sets "Disjoint sets"), then translate and rotate these sets to form two identical copies of the same ball; this is the [Banach–Tarski paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Banach%E2%80%93Tarski_paradox "Banach–Tarski paradox"). They also proved that a two-dimensional disk has no such paradoxical decomposition. But in 1929,[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-93) von Neumann subdivided the disk into finitely many pieces and rearranged them into two disks, using area-preserving [affine transformations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Affine_transformation "Affine transformation") instead of translations and rotations. The result depended on finding [free groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_group "Free group") of affine transformations, an important technique extended later by von Neumann in [his work on measure theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#Measure_theory).[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUlam195814%E2%80%9315-94)

With the contributions of von Neumann to sets, the axiomatic system of the theory of sets avoided the contradictions of earlier systems and became usable as a foundation for mathematics, despite the lack of a proof of its [consistency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consistency "Consistency"). The next question was whether it provided definitive answers to all mathematical questions that could be posed in it, or whether it might be improved by adding stronger [axioms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom#Mathematical_logic "Axiom") that could be used to prove a broader class of theorems.[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-95)

By 1927, von Neumann was involving himself in discussions in Göttingen on whether [elementary arithmetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elementary_arithmetic "Elementary arithmetic") followed from [Peano axioms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peano_axioms "Peano axioms").[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-96) Building on the work of [Ackermann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Ackermann "Wilhelm Ackermann"), he began attempting to prove (using the [finistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Finitism "Finitism") methods of [Hilbert's school](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert%27s_program "Hilbert's program")) the consistency of [first-order arithmetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peano_axioms#Peano_arithmetic_as_first-order_theory "Peano axioms"). He succeeded in proving the consistency of a fragment of arithmetic of natural numbers (through the use of restrictions on [induction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_induction "Mathematical induction")).[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-97) He continued looking for a more general proof of the consistency of classical mathematics using methods from [proof theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proof_theory "Proof theory").[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurawski2010204%E2%80%93206-98)

A strongly negative answer to whether it was definitive arrived in September 1930 at the [Second Conference on the Epistemology of the Exact Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Conference_on_the_Epistemology_of_the_Exact_Sciences "Second Conference on the Epistemology of the Exact Sciences"), in which [Kurt Gödel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kurt_G%C3%B6del "Kurt Gödel") announced his [first theorem of incompleteness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_incompleteness_theorems "Gödel's incompleteness theorems"): the usual axiomatic systems are incomplete, in the sense that they cannot prove every truth expressible in their language. Moreover, every consistent extension of these systems necessarily remains incomplete.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTER%C3%A9dei2005123-99) At the conference, von Neumann suggested to Gödel that he should try to transform his results for undecidable propositions about integers.[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Plato20184080-100)

Less than a month later, von Neumann communicated to Gödel an interesting consequence of his theorem: the usual axiomatic systems are unable to demonstrate their own consistency.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTER%C3%A9dei2005123-99) Gödel replied that he had already discovered this consequence, now known as his [second incompleteness theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_incompleteness_theorem "Second incompleteness theorem"), and that he would send a preprint of his article containing both results, which never appeared.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-101)[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Plato20184083%E2%80%934088-102)[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Plato202024%E2%80%9328-103) Von Neumann acknowledged Gödel's priority in his next letter.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTER%C3%A9dei2005124-104) However, von Neumann's method of proof differed from Gödel's, and he was also of the opinion that the second incompleteness theorem had dealt a much stronger blow to Hilbert's program than Gödel thought it did.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Plato202022-105)[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-106) With this discovery, which drastically changed his views on mathematical rigor, von Neumann ceased research in the [foundations of mathematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics "Foundations of mathematics") and [metamathematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metamathematics "Metamathematics") and instead spent time on problems connected with applications.[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurawski2010209-107)

In a series of papers published in 1932, von Neumann made foundational contributions to [ergodic theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ergodic_theory "Ergodic theory"), a branch of mathematics that involves the states of [dynamical systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamical_systems "Dynamical systems") with an [invariant measure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invariant_measure "Invariant measure").[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-108) Of the 1932 papers on ergodic theory, [Paul Halmos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Halmos "Paul Halmos") wrote that even "if von Neumann had never done anything else, they would have been sufficient to guarantee him mathematical immortality".[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalmos195893-109) By then von Neumann had already written his articles on [operator theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operator_theory "Operator theory"), and the application of this work was instrumental in his [mean ergodic theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ergodic_theory#Mean_ergodic_theorem "Ergodic theory").[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalmos195891-110)

The theorem is about arbitrary [one-parameter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/One-parameter_group "One-parameter group")[unitary groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unitary_group "Unitary group")![Image 6: {\displaystyle {\mathit {t}}\to {\mathit {V_{t}}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/02d6ba6801863bc8d4ca1cd8ffa6fa02cf0e5f2a) and states that for every vector ![Image 7: {\displaystyle \phi }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/72b1f30316670aee6270a28334bdf4f5072cdde4) in the [Hilbert space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert_space "Hilbert space"), ![Image 8: {\textstyle \lim _{T\to \infty }{\frac {1}{T}}\int _{0}^{T}V_{t}(\phi )\,dt}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3a826261232ba8b533accb618b1b3964014ba977) exists in the sense of the metric defined by the Hilbert norm and is a vector ![Image 9: {\displaystyle \psi }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/45e5789e5d9c8f7c79744f43ecaaf8ba42a8553a) which is such that ![Image 10: {\displaystyle V_{t}(\psi )=\psi }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bff8f6882410e1d37f997787cba4da726af52a00) for all ![Image 11: {\displaystyle t}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/65658b7b223af9e1acc877d848888ecdb4466560). This was proven in the first paper. In the second paper, von Neumann argued that his results here were sufficient for physical applications relating to [Boltzmann's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann "Ludwig Boltzmann")[ergodic hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ergodic_hypothesis "Ergodic hypothesis"). He also pointed out that [ergodicity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ergodicity "Ergodicity") had not yet been achieved and isolated this for future work.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-mackey1990-111)

Later in the year he published another influential paper that began the systematic study of ergodicity. He gave and proved a decomposition theorem showing that the ergodic [measure preserving actions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Measure-preserving_dynamical_system "Measure-preserving dynamical system") of the real line are the fundamental building blocks from which all measure preserving actions can be built. Several other key theorems are given and proven. The results in this paper and another in conjunction with [Paul Halmos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Halmos "Paul Halmos") have significant applications in other areas of mathematics.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-mackey1990-111)[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-112)

In [measure theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Measure_(mathematics) "Measure (mathematics)"), the "problem of measure" for an n-dimensional [Euclidean space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclidean_space "Euclidean space")**R**_n_ may be stated as: "does there exist a positive, normalized, invariant, and additive set function on the class of all subsets of **R**_n_?"[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalmos195886-113) The work of [Felix Hausdorff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Felix_Hausdorff "Felix Hausdorff") and [Stefan Banach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stefan_Banach "Stefan Banach") had implied that the problem of measure has a positive solution if _n_ = 1 or _n_ = 2 and a negative solution (because of the [Banach–Tarski paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Banach%E2%80%93Tarski_paradox "Banach–Tarski paradox")) in all other cases. Von Neumann's work argued that the "problem is essentially group-theoretic in character": the existence of a measure could be determined by looking at the properties of the [transformation group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transformation_group "Transformation group") of the given space. The positive solution for spaces of dimension at most two, and the negative solution for higher dimensions, comes from the fact that the [Euclidean group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclidean_group "Euclidean group") is a [solvable group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solvable_group "Solvable group") for dimension at most two, and is not solvable for higher dimensions. "Thus, according to von Neumann, it is the change of group that makes a difference, not the change of space."[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalmos195887-114) Around 1942 he told [Dorothy Maharam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dorothy_Maharam "Dorothy Maharam") how to prove that every [complete](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complete_measure "Complete measure")[σ-finite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%A3-finite_measure "Σ-finite measure")[measure space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Measure_space "Measure space") has a multiplicative lifting; he did not publish this proof and she later came up with a new one.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch2007168-115)

In a number of von Neumann's papers, the methods of argument he employed are considered even more significant than the results. In anticipation of his later study of dimension theory in algebras of operators, von Neumann used results on equivalence by finite decomposition, and reformulated the problem of measure in terms of functions.[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalmos195888-116) A major contribution von Neumann made to measure theory was the result of a paper written to answer a question of [Haar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfr%C3%A9d_Haar "Alfréd Haar") regarding whether there existed an [algebra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebra_over_a_field "Algebra over a field") of all bounded functions on the real number line such that they form "a complete system of representatives of the classes of almost everywhere-equal measurable bounded functions".[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieudonn%C3%A92008-117) He proved this in the positive, and in later papers with [Stone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marshall_Harvey_Stone "Marshall Harvey Stone") discussed various generalizations and algebraic aspects of this problem.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-118) He also proved by new methods the existence of [disintegrations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disintegration_theorem "Disintegration theorem") for various general types of measures. Von Neumann also gave a new proof on the uniqueness of Haar measures by using the mean values of functions, although this method only worked for [compact groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compact_group "Compact group").[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieudonn%C3%A92008-117) He had to create entirely new techniques to apply this to [locally compact groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Locally_compact_group "Locally compact group").[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalmos195889-119) He also gave a new, ingenious proof for the [Radon–Nikodym theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radon%E2%80%93Nikodym_theorem "Radon–Nikodym theorem").[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-120) His lecture notes on measure theory at the Institute for Advanced Study were an important source for knowledge on the topic in America at the time, and were later published.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalmos195890-121)[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-122)[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-123)

Using his previous work on measure theory, von Neumann made several contributions to the theory of [topological groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topological_group "Topological group"), beginning with a paper on almost periodic functions on groups, where von Neumann extended [Bohr's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harald_Bohr "Harald Bohr") theory of [almost periodic functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Almost_periodic_function "Almost periodic function") to arbitrary [groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Group_(mathematics) "Group (mathematics)").[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-124) He continued this work with another paper in conjunction with [Bochner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salomon_Bochner "Salomon Bochner") that improved the theory of almost [periodicity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Periodic_function "Periodic function") to include [functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Function_(mathematics) "Function (mathematics)") that took on elements of [linear spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_space "Vector space") as values rather than numbers.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-125) In 1938, he was awarded the [Bôcher Memorial Prize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/B%C3%B4cher_Memorial_Prize "Bôcher Memorial Prize") for his work in [analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_analysis "Mathematical analysis") in relation to these papers.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-126)[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBochner1958440-127)

In a 1933 paper, he used the newly discovered [Haar measure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haar_measure "Haar measure") in the solution of [Hilbert's fifth problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert%27s_fifth_problem "Hilbert's fifth problem") for the case of [compact groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compact_group "Compact group").[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-128) The basic idea behind this was discovered several years earlier when von Neumann published a paper on the analytic properties of groups of [linear transformations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_map "Linear map") and found that closed [subgroups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subgroup "Subgroup") of a general [linear group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_group "Linear group") are [Lie groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lie_group "Lie group").[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-129) This was later extended by [Cartan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%89lie_Cartan "Élie Cartan") to arbitrary Lie groups in the form of the [closed-subgroup theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Closed-subgroup_theorem "Closed-subgroup theorem").[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBochner1958441-130)[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieudonn%C3%A92008-117)

### Functional analysis

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=John_von_Neumann&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Functional analysis")]

Von Neumann was the first to axiomatically define an abstract [Hilbert space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert_space "Hilbert space"). He defined it as a [complex vector space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_space "Vector space") with a [Hermitian scalar product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inner_product_space "Inner product space"), with the corresponding [norm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norm_(mathematics) "Norm (mathematics)") being both separable and complete. In the same papers he also proved the general form of the [Cauchy–Schwarz inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cauchy%E2%80%93Schwarz_inequality "Cauchy–Schwarz inequality") that had previously been known only in specific examples.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch200711-131) He continued with the development of the [spectral theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spectral_theory "Spectral theory") of operators in Hilbert space in three seminal papers between 1929 and 1932.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieudonn%C3%A91981172-132) This work cumulated in his _[Mathematical Foundations of Quantum Mechanics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical\_Foundations\_of\_Quantum\_Mechanics "Mathematical Foundations of Quantum Mechanics")_ which alongside two other books by [Stone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marshall_Harvey_Stone "Marshall Harvey Stone") and [Banach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stefan_Banach "Stefan Banach") in the same year were the first monographs on Hilbert space theory.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch200714-133) Previous work by others showed that a theory of [weak topologies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weak_topology "Weak topology") could not be obtained by using [sequences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weak_convergence_(Hilbert_space) "Weak convergence (Hilbert space)"). Von Neumann was the first to outline a program of how to overcome the difficulties, which resulted in him defining [locally convex spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Locally_convex_topological_vector_space "Locally convex topological vector space") and [topological vector spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topological_vector_spaces "Topological vector spaces") for the first time. In addition several other topological properties he defined at the time (he was among the first mathematicians to apply new topological ideas from [Hausdorff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Felix_Hausdorff "Felix Hausdorff") from Euclidean to Hilbert spaces)[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieudonn%C3%A91981211,_218-134) such as [boundness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bounded_set_(topological_vector_space) "Bounded set (topological vector space)") and [total boundness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Totally_bounded_space "Totally bounded space") are still used today.[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch200758,_65%E2%80%9366-135) For twenty years von Neumann was considered the 'undisputed master' of this area.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieudonn%C3%A92008-117) These developments were primarily prompted by needs in [quantum mechanics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_mechanics "Quantum mechanics") where von Neumann realized the need to extend [the spectral theory of Hermitian operators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-adjoint_operator#Spectral_theorem "Self-adjoint operator") from the bounded to the [unbounded](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unbounded_operator "Unbounded operator") case.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-steen-136) Other major achievements in these papers include a complete elucidation of spectral theory for [normal operators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Normal_operator "Normal operator"), the first abstract presentation of the [trace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trace_(linear_algebra) "Trace (linear algebra)") of a [positive operator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positive_operator_(Hilbert_space) "Positive operator (Hilbert space)"),[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-137)[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTELordSukochevZanin20121-138) a generalisation of [Riesz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frigyes_Riesz "Frigyes Riesz")'s presentation of [Hilbert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Hilbert "David Hilbert")'s spectral theorems at the time, and the discovery of [Hermitian operators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-adjoint_operator#Definitions "Self-adjoint operator") in a Hilbert space, as distinct from [self-adjoint operators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-adjoint_operator "Self-adjoint operator"), which enabled him to give a description of all Hermitian operators which extend a given Hermitian operator. He wrote a paper detailing how the usage of [infinite matrices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)#Infinite_matrices "Matrix (mathematics)"), common at the time in spectral theory, was inadequate as a representation for Hermitian operators. His work on operator theory lead to his most profound invention in pure mathematics, the study of von Neumann algebras and in general of [operator algebras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operator_algebra "Operator algebra").[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieudonn%C3%A91981175%E2%80%93176,_178%E2%80%93179,_181,_183-139)

His later work on rings of operators lead to him revisiting his work on spectral theory and providing a new way of working through the geometric content by the use of direct integrals of Hilbert spaces.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-steen-136) Like in his work on measure theory he proved several theorems that he did not find time to publish. He told [Nachman Aronszajn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nachman_Aronszajn "Nachman Aronszajn") and K. T. Smith that in the early 1930s he proved the existence of proper invariant subspaces for completely continuous operators in a Hilbert space while working on the [invariant subspace problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invariant_subspace_problem "Invariant subspace problem").[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch2007202-140)

With [I. J. Schoenberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Jacob_Schoenberg "Isaac Jacob Schoenberg") he wrote several items investigating [translation invariant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Translational_symmetry "Translational symmetry") Hilbertian [metrics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metric_(mathematics) "Metric (mathematics)") on the [real number line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number_line "Number line") which resulted in their complete classification. Their motivation lie in various questions related to embedding [metric spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metric_space "Metric space") into Hilbert spaces.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-141)[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-142)

With [Pascual Jordan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascual_Jordan "Pascual Jordan") he wrote a short paper giving the first derivation of a given norm from an [inner product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inner_product_space "Inner product space") by means of the [parallelogram identity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallelogram_law#The_parallelogram_law_in_inner_product_spaces "Parallelogram law").[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHornJohnson2013320-143) His [trace inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trace_inequality#Von_Neumann's_trace_inequality_and_related_results "Trace inequality") is a key result of matrix theory used in matrix approximation problems.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHornJohnson2013458-144) He also first presented the idea that the dual of a pre-norm is a norm in the first major paper discussing the theory of unitarily invariant norms and symmetric gauge functions (now known as symmetric absolute norms).[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-145)[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHornJohnson2013335-146)[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-147) This paper leads naturally to the study of symmetric [operator ideals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operator_ideal "Operator ideal") and is the beginning point for modern studies of symmetric [operator spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operator_space "Operator space").[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTELordSukochevZanin202173-148)

Later with [Robert Schatten](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Schatten "Robert Schatten") he initiated the study of [nuclear operators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_operator "Nuclear operator") on Hilbert spaces,[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-149)[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-150)[tensor products of Banach spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topological_tensor_product#Cross_norms_and_tensor_products_of_Banach_spaces "Topological tensor product"),[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch2007372-151) introduced and studied [trace class](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trace_class "Trace class") operators,[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch201454-152) their [ideals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideal_(ring_theory) "Ideal (ring theory)"), and their [duality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duality_(mathematics) "Duality (mathematics)") with [compact operators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compact_operator "Compact operator"), and [preduality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predual "Predual") with [bounded operators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bounded_operator "Bounded operator").[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTELordSukochevZanin201273-153) The generalization of this topic to the study of [nuclear operators on Banach spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_operators_between_Banach_spaces "Nuclear operators between Banach spaces") was among the first achievements of [Alexander Grothendieck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Grothendieck "Alexander Grothendieck").[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTELordSukochevZanin202126-154)[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch2007272-155) Previously in 1937 von Neumann published several results in this area, for example giving 1-parameter scale of different cross norms on ![Image 12: {\displaystyle {\textit {l}}\,_{2}^{n}\otimes {\textit {l}}\,_{2}^{n}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fb05e781d19ea8f168d193c27baed308d7eb69c9) and proving several other results on what are now known as Schatten–von Neumann ideals.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch2007272,_338-156)

Von Neumann founded the study of rings of operators, through the [von Neumann algebras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von_Neumann_algebra "Von Neumann algebra") (originally called W*-algebras). While his original ideas for [rings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noncommutative_ring "Noncommutative ring") of [operators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_map "Linear map") existed already in 1930, he did not begin studying them in depth until he met [F. J. Murray](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francis_Joseph_Murray "Francis Joseph Murray") several years later.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch2007140-157)[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-158) A von Neumann algebra is a [*-algebra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/*-algebra "*-algebra") of bounded operators on a [Hilbert space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert_space "Hilbert space") that is closed in the [weak operator topology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weak_operator_topology "Weak operator topology") and contains the [identity operator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Identity_function "Identity function").[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-159) The [von Neumann bicommutant theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von_Neumann_bicommutant_theorem "Von Neumann bicommutant theorem") shows that the analytic definition is equivalent to a purely algebraic definition as being equal to the [bicommutant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bicommutant "Bicommutant").[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-160) After elucidating the study of the [commutative algebra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commutative_ring "Commutative ring") case, von Neumann embarked in 1936, with the partial collaboration of Murray, on the [noncommutative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noncommutative_ring "Noncommutative ring") case, the general study of [factors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von_Neumann_algebra "Von Neumann algebra") classification of von Neumann algebras. The six major papers in which he developed that theory between 1936 and 1940 "rank among the masterpieces of analysis in the twentieth century";[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDieudonn%C3%A9200890-161) they collect many foundational results and started several programs in operator algebra theory that mathematicians worked on for decades afterwards. An example is the classification of [factors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von_Neumann_algebra#Factors "Von Neumann algebra").[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch2007151-162) In addition in 1938 he proved that every von Neumann algebra on a separable Hilbert space is a direct integral of factors; he did not find time to publish this result until 1949.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch2007146-163)[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-164) Von Neumann algebras relate closely to a theory of noncommutative integration, something that von Neumann hinted to in his work but did not explicitly write out.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTESegal1965-165)[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-166) Another important result on [polar decomposition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_decomposition "Polar decomposition") was published in 1932.[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietsch2007148-167)

Between 1935 and 1937, von Neumann worked on [lattice theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lattice_(order) "Lattice (order)"), the theory of [partially ordered sets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partially_ordered_set "Partially ordered set") in which every two elements have a greatest lower bound and a least upper bound. As [Garrett Birkhoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Garrett_Birkhoff "Garrett Birkhoff") wrote, "John von Neumann's brilliant mind blazed over lattice theory like a meteor".[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirkhoff195850-168) Von Neumann combined traditional projective geometry with modern algebra ([linear algebra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_algebra "Linear algebra"), [ring theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ring_(mathematics) "Ring (mathematics)"), lattice theory). Many previously geometric results could then be interpreted in the case of general [modules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Module_(mathematics) "Module (mathematics)") over rings. His work laid the foundations for some of the modern work in projective geometry.[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-lashkhi1995-169)

His biggest contribution was founding the field of [continuous geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_geometry "Continuous geometry").[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-170) It followed his path-breaking work on rings of operators. In mathematics, continuous geometry is a substitute of complex [projective geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Projective_geometry "Projective geometry"), where instead of the [dimension](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dimension_(vector_space) "Dimension (vector space)") of a [subspace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_subspace "Linear subspace") being in a discrete set ![Image 13: {\displaystyle 0,1,...,{\mathit {n}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/eaffd696d9ff3886d8df8e3ef79c67d70939512c) it can be an element of the [unit interval](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_interval "Unit interval")![Image 14: {\displaystyle [0,1]}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/738f7d23bb2d9642bab520020873cccbef49768d). Earlier, [Menger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Menger "Karl Menger") and Birkhoff had axiomatized [complex projective geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_projective_space "Complex projective space") in terms of the properties of its [lattice of linear subspaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_subspace#Lattice_of_subspaces "Linear subspace"). Von Neumann, following his work on rings of operators, weakened those [axioms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom "Axiom") to describe a broader class of lattices, the continuous geometries.

While the dimensions of the subspaces of projective geometries are a discrete set (the [non-negative integers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_number "Natural number")), the dimensions of the elements of a continuous geometry can range continuously across the unit interval ![Image 15: {\displaystyle [0,1]}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/738f7d23bb2d9642bab520020873cccbef49768d). Von Neumann was motivated by his discovery of [von Neumann algebras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von_Neumann_algebra "Von Neumann algebra") with a dimension function taking a continuous range of dimensions, and the first example of a continuous geometry other than projective space was the [projections](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von_Neumann_algebra#Projections "Von Neumann algebra") of the [hyperfinite type II factor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperfinite_type_II_factor "Hyperfinite type II factor").[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacrae1992140-171)[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-172)

In more pure lattice theoretical work, he solved the difficult problem of characterizing the class of ![Image 16: {\displaystyle {\mathit {CG(F)}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d72ce8e9dc3142fce1bccf6090e42ff8c2cca536) (continuous-dimensional projective geometry over an arbitrary [division ring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Division_ring "Division ring")![Image 17: {\displaystyle {\mathit {F}}\,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f60bcaf05734448407532f0f758690f69d64812c)) in abstract language of lattice theory.[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirkhoff195850%E2%80%9351-173) Von Neumann provided an abstract exploration of dimension in completed [complemented](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complemented_lattice "Complemented lattice")[modular](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_lattice "Modular lattice") topological lattices (properties that arise in the [lattices of subspaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_subspace#Lattice_of_subspaces "Linear subspace") of [inner product spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inner_product_space "Inner product space")):

> Dimension is determined, up to a positive linear transformation, by the following two properties. It is conserved by perspective mappings ("perspectivities") and ordered by inclusion. The deepest part of the proof concerns the equivalence of perspectivity with "projectivity by decomposition"—of which a corollary is the transitivity of perspectivity.

For any integer ![Image 18: {\displaystyle n>3}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/257030caae597fd034c2cbcff2cff9dfc4272d20) every ![Image 19: {\displaystyle {\mathit {n}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f8379ab758c42519867fc601fa155ea3175a05b3)-dimensional abstract projective geometry is [isomorphic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isomorphism "Isomorphism") to the subspace-lattice of an ![Image 20: {\displaystyle {\mathit {n}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f8379ab758c42519867fc601fa155ea3175a05b3)-dimensional [vector space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_space "Vector space")![Image 21: {\displaystyle V_{n}(F)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f22dd66202178c3412b182665f202a60aea9d9bf) over a (unique) corresponding division ring ![Image 22: {\displaystyle F}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/545fd099af8541605f7ee55f08225526be88ce57). This is known as the [Veblen–Young theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Veblen%E2%80%93Young_theorem "Veblen–Young theorem"). Von Neumann extended this fundamental result in projective geometry to the continuous dimensional case.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirkhoff195851-174) This [coordinatization theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_geometry#Coordinatization_theorem "Continuous geometry") stimulated considerable work in abstract projective geometry and lattice theory, much of which continued using von Neumann's techniques.[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-lashkhi1995-169)[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-175) Birkhoff described this theorem as follows:

> Any complemented modular lattice L having a "basis" of _n_ ≥ 4 pairwise perspective elements, is isomorphic with the lattice ℛ(_R_) of all principal [right-ideals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideal_(ring_theory) "Ideal (ring theory)") of a suitable [regular ring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von_Neumann_regular_ring "Von Neumann regular ring")R. This conclusion is the culmination of 140 pages of brilliant and incisive algebra involving entirely novel axioms. Anyone wishing to get an unforgettable impression of the razor edge of von Neumann's mind, need merely try to pursue this chain of exact reasoning for himself—realizing that often five pages of it were written down before breakfast, seated at a living room writing-table in a bathrobe.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirkhoff195852-176)

This work required the creation of [regular rings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von_Neumann_regular_ring "Von Neumann regular ring").[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-177) A von Neumann regular ring is a [ring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ring_(mathematics) "Ring (mathematics)") where for every ![Image 23: {\displaystyle a}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ffd2487510aa438433a2579450ab2b3d557e5edc), an element ![Image 24: {\displaystyle x}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4) exists such that ![Image 25: {\displaystyle axa=a}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9087a34969db199503b1f5dd76cf7848112ce767).[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirkhoff195852-176) These rings came from and have connections to his work on von Neumann algebras, as well as [AW*-algebras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AW*-algebra "AW*-algebra") and various kinds of [C*-algebras](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C*-algebra "C*-algebra").[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-178)

Many smaller technical results were proven during the creation and proof of the above theorems, particularly regarding [distributivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Distributive_property "Distributive property") (such as infinite distributivity), von Neumann developing them as needed. He also developed a theory of valuations in lattices, and shared in developing the general theory of [metric lattices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metric_lattice "Metric lattice").[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirkhoff195852%E2%80%9353-179)

Birkhoff noted in his posthumous article on von Neumann that most of these results were developed in an intense two-year period of work, and that while his interests continued in lattice theory after 1937, they became peripheral and mainly occurred in letters to other mathematicians. A final contribution in 1940 was for a joint seminar he conducted with Birkhoff at the Institute for Advanced Study on the subject where he developed a theory of σ-complete lattice ordered rings. He never wrote up the work for publication.[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBirkhoff195855%E2%80%9356-180)

### Mathematical statistics

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=John_von_Neumann&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Mathematical statistics")]

Von Neumann made fundamental contributions to [mathematical statistics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_statistics "Mathematical statistics"). In 1941, he derived the exact distribution of the ratio of the mean square of successive differences to the sample variance for independent and identically [normally](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Normal_distribution "Normal distribution") distributed variables.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-181) This ratio was applied to the residuals from regression models and is commonly known as the [Durbin–Watson statistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Durbin%E2%80%93Watson_statistic "Durbin–Watson statistic")[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-jstor.org-182) for testing the null hypothesis that the errors are serially independent against the alternative that they follow a stationary first order [autoregression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autoregressive_model "Autoregressive model").[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-jstor.org-182)

Subsequently, [Denis Sargan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Denis_Sargan "Denis Sargan") and [Alok Bhargava](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alok_Bhargava "Alok Bhargava") extended the results for testing whether the errors on a regression model follow a Gaussian [random walk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Random_walk "Random walk") (_i.e._, possess a [unit root](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_root "Unit root")) against the alternative that they are a stationary first order autoregression.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann#cite_note-183)
